<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">The prevailing cosmological model for the observable universe</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Big Bang theory" redirects here. For the American TV sitcom, see <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Bang_Theory" title="The Big Bang Theory">The Big Bang Theory</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Big_Bang_(disambiguation)" title="Big Bang (disambiguation)">Big Bang (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Big_Bang_Theory_(disambiguation)" title="Big Bang Theory (disambiguation)">Big Bang Theory (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1980" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg/330px-CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg/495px-CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg/660px-CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CMB_Timeline300_no_WMAP.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Timeline of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">metric expansion of space</a>, where space (including hypothetical non-observable portions of the universe) is represented at each time by the circular sections. On the left, the dramatic expansion occurs in the <a href="/wiki/Inflationary_epoch" title="Inflationary epoch">inflationary epoch</a>; and at the center, the expansion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accelerating_universe" title="Accelerating universe">accelerates</a> (artist\'s concept; not to scale).</div></div></div>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks plainlist" Probe">Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy<br/>Probe (WMAP)</a></div></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:#ddd8e7;text-align:center;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_cosmologists" title="List of cosmologists">Scientists</a></li></ul></div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center"><div class="hlist" style="padding:0 0.9em;">\n<li><a Copernicus">Copernicus</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Willem_de_Sitter" title="Willem de Sitter">de Sitter</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Dicke" title="Robert H. Tolman">Tolman</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Robert_Woodrow_Wilson" title="Robert Woodrow Wilson">Wilson</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Yakov_Zeldovich" title="Yakov Zeldovich">Zeldovich</a></li>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:#ddd8e7;text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology#Subject_history" title="Physical cosmology">Subject history</a></div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><div style="padding:0.2em 0.4em; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Discovery_of_cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Discovery of cosmic microwave background radiation">Discovery of cosmic microwave<br/>background radiation</a></div></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Big_Bang_theory" title="History of the Big Bang theory">History of the Big Bang theory</a></li>\n<li><div style="padding:0.2em 0.4em; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Religious_interpretations_of_the_Big_Bang_theory" title="Religious interpretations of the Big Bang theory">Religious interpretations of<br/>the Big Bang theory</a></div></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_cosmological_theories" title="Timeline of cosmological theories">Timeline of cosmological theories</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;display:block;margin-top:0.4em; line-height:1.6em;padding-bottom:0.5em;">\n<ul><li><img alt="Category" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="36" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/16px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/24px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/32px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Category:Physical_cosmology" title="Category:Physical cosmology">Category</a></li>\n<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png"><img alt="Ilc 9yr moll4096.png" class="noviewer" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="4096" decoding="async" height="8" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png/16px-Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png/24px-Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png/32px-Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Cosmology" title="Portal:Cosmology">Cosmology portal</a></li>\n<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crab_Nebula.jpg"><img alt="Crab Nebula.jpg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="3864" data-file-width="3864" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/16px-Crab_Nebula.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/24px-Crab_Nebula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/32px-Crab_Nebula.jpg 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Astronomy" title="Portal:Astronomy">Astronomy portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Physical_cosmology" title="Template:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Physical_cosmology" title="Template talk:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Big Bang</b> <a href="/wiki/Scientific_theory" title="Scientific theory">theory</a> is the prevailing <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">cosmological</a> <a href="/wiki/Scientific_modelling" title="Scientific modelling">model</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170220_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170220-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> from the <a href="/wiki/Planck_units#Cosmology" title="Planck units">earliest known periods</a> through its subsequent large-scale evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The model describes how the universe <a href="/wiki/Expansion_of_the_universe" title="Expansion of the universe">expanded</a> from a very high-density and high-temperature state,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTUW_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-HTUW-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and offers a comprehensive explanation for a broad range of phenomena, including the abundance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_element" title="Light element">light elements</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a> (CMB), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_cosmos" title="Large-scale structure of the cosmos">large scale structure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble\'s law">Hubble\'s law</a> (the farther away galaxies are, the faster they are moving away from Earth).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright2009_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wright2009-9">[9]</a></sup> If the observed conditions are extrapolated backwards in time using the known laws of physics, the prediction is that just before a period of very high density there was a <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_singularity" title="Gravitational singularity">singularity</a> which is typically associated with the Big Bang. Physicists are undecided whether this means the universe began from a singularity, or that current knowledge is insufficient to describe the universe at that time. Detailed measurements of the expansion rate of the universe place the Big Bang at around 13.8 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1,000,000,000_(number)" title="1,000,000,000 (number)">billion</a> years ago, which is thus considered the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">age of the universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa-10">[10]</a></sup> After its initial expansion, the universe cooled sufficiently to allow the formation of <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particles</a>, and later simple <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>. Giant clouds of these primordial elements (mostly <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, with some <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>) later coalesced through <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, eventually forming early <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a>, the descendants of which are visible today. Astronomers also observe the gravitational effects of <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> surrounding galaxies. Though most of the mass in the universe seems to be in the form of dark matter, Big Bang theory and various observations seem to indicate that it is not made out of conventional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baryonic_matter" title="Baryonic matter">baryonic matter</a> (protons, neutrons, and electrons) but it is unclear exactly what it <i>is</i> made out of.\n</p><p>Since <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lema&#238;tre">Georges Lema&#238;tre</a> first noted in 1927 that an expanding universe could be traced back in time to an originating single point, scientists have built on his idea of cosmic expansion. The scientific community was once divided between supporters of two different theories, the Big Bang and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steady_State_theory" title="Steady State theory">Steady State theory</a>, but a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Empirical_evidence" title="Empirical evidence">empirical evidence</a> has strongly favored the Big Bang which is now universally accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kragh_1996_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kragh_1996-11">[11]</a></sup> In 1929, from analysis of galactic <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshifts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a> concluded that galaxies are drifting apart; this is important observational evidence consistent with the hypothesis of an expanding universe. In 1964, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">cosmic microwave background radiation</a> was discovered, which was crucial evidence in favor of the Big Bang model,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> since that theory predicted the existence of background radiation throughout the universe before it was discovered. More recently, measurements of the redshifts of supernovae indicate that the <a href="/wiki/Accelerating_expansion_of_the_universe" title="Accelerating expansion of the universe">expansion of the universe is accelerating</a>, an observation attributed to <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>\'s existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peebles_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-peebles-13">[13]</a></sup> The known <a href="/wiki/Physical_law" title="Physical law">physical laws of nature</a> can be used to calculate the characteristics of the universe in detail back in time to an initial state of extreme <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> and <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Religious and philosophical interpretations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Books"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Books</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span></h2>\n<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; clear:right; margin: 0.5em 0em 0.5em 0.5em">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><img alt="External Timeline" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Timeline_icon.svg/50px-Timeline_icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Timeline_icon.svg/75px-Timeline_icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Timeline_icon.svg/100px-Timeline_icon.svg.png 2x" title="External Timeline" width="50"/>\n</td>\n<td width="215px">A graphical timeline is available at<br/><i><a href="/wiki/Graphical_timeline_of_the_Big_Bang" title="Graphical timeline of the Big Bang">Graphical timeline of the Big Bang</a></i>\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<p>The Belgian astronomer and Catholic priest <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lema&#238;tre">Georges Lema&#238;tre</a> proposed on theoretical grounds that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">universe is expanding</a>, which was observationally confirmed soon afterwards by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> In 1927 in the <i>Annales de la Soci&#233;t&#233; Scientifique de Bruxelles</i> (<i>Annals of the Scientific Society of Brussels</i>) under the title "Un Univers homog&#232;ne de masse constante et de rayon croissant rendant compte de la vitesse radiale des n&#233;buleuses extragalactiques" ("A homogeneous Universe of constant mass and growing radius accounting for the radial velocity of extragalactic nebulae"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> he presented his new idea that the universe is expanding and provided the first observational estimation of what is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_constant" title="Hubble constant">Hubble constant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> What later will be known as the "Big Bang theory" of the origin of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>, he called his "hypothesis of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primeval_atom" title="Primeval atom">primeval atom</a>" or the "Cosmic Egg".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>American astronomer Edwin Hubble observed that the distances to faraway galaxies were strongly correlated with their <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshifts</a>. This was interpreted to mean that all distant galaxies and clusters are receding away from our vantage point with an apparent velocity proportional to their distance: that is, the farther they are, the faster they move away from us, regardless of direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubble_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-hubble-20">[20]</a></sup> Assuming the <a href="/wiki/Copernican_principle" title="Copernican principle">Copernican principle</a> (that the Earth is not the center of the universe), the only remaining interpretation is that all observable regions of the universe are receding from all others. Since we know that the distance between galaxies increases today, it must mean that in the past galaxies were closer together. The continuous expansion of the universe implies that the universe was denser and hotter in the past.\n</p><p>Large <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerators</a> can replicate the conditions that prevailed after the early moments of the universe, resulting in confirmation and refinement of the details of the Big Bang model. However, these accelerators can only probe so far into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-energy_physics" title="High-energy physics">high energy regimes</a>. Consequently, the state of the universe in the earliest instants of the Big Bang expansion is still poorly understood and an area of open investigation and speculation.\n</p><p>The first <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particles</a> to be formed included <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>. Though simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big bang nucleosynthesis">atomic nuclei formed</a> within the first three minutes after the Big Bang, thousands of years passed before the first <a href="/wiki/Recombination_(cosmology)" title="Recombination (cosmology)">electrically neutral atoms formed</a>. The majority of atoms produced by the Big Bang were <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> and traces of <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>. Giant clouds of these primordial elements later coalesced through <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> and galaxies, and the heavier elements were synthesized either <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">within stars</a> or <a href="/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis" title="Supernova nucleosynthesis">during supernovae</a>.\n</p><p>The Big Bang theory offers a comprehensive explanation for a broad range of observed phenomena, including the abundance of light elements, the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">CMB</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_cosmos" title="Large-scale structure of the cosmos">large scale structure</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_Law" title="Hubble\'s Law">Hubble\'s Law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright2009_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wright2009-9">[9]</a></sup> The framework for the Big Bang model relies on <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>\'s theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> and on simplifying assumptions such as <a href="/wiki/Homogeneity_(physics)#Translation_invariance" title="Homogeneity (physics)">homogeneity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isotropy" title="Isotropy">isotropy</a> of space. The governing equations were formulated by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Friedmann" title="Alexander Friedmann">Alexander Friedmann</a>, and similar solutions were worked on by <a href="/wiki/Willem_de_Sitter" title="Willem de Sitter">Willem de Sitter</a>. Since then, astrophysicists have incorporated observational and theoretical additions into the Big Bang model, and its <a href="/wiki/Parametric_equation" title="Parametric equation">parametrization</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">Lambda-CDM model</a> serves as the framework for current investigations of theoretical cosmology. The Lambda-CDM model is the current "standard model" of Big Bang cosmology, <a href="/wiki/Scientific_consensus" title="Scientific consensus">consensus</a> is that it is the simplest model that can account for the various measurements and <a href="/wiki/Observational_cosmology" title="Observational cosmology">observations relevant to cosmology</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">Timeline</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_universe" title="Chronology of the universe">Chronology of the universe</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Singularity">Singularity</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_singularity" title="Gravitational singularity">Gravitational singularity</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck_epoch" title="Planck epoch">Planck epoch</a></div>\n<p>Extrapolation of the expansion of the universe backwards in time using general relativity yields an <a href="/wiki/Infinity#Physics" title="Infinity">infinite</a> <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> and temperature at a finite time in the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_singularity" title="Gravitational singularity">singularity</a> indicates that <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> is not an adequate description of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laws_of_physics" title="Laws of physics">laws of physics</a> in this regime. Models based on general relativity alone can not extrapolate toward the singularity beyond the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck_epoch" title="Planck epoch">Planck epoch</a>.\n</p><p>This primordial singularity is itself sometimes called "the Big Bang",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> but the term can also refer to a more generic early hot, dense phase<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[notes 1]</a></sup> of the universe. In either case, "the Big Bang" as an event is also colloquially referred to as the "birth" of our universe since it represents the point in history where the universe can be verified to have entered into a <a href="/wiki/Equation_of_state_(cosmology)" title="Equation of state (cosmology)">regime</a> where the laws of physics as we understand them (specifically general relativity and the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">standard model of particle physics</a>) work. Based on measurements of the expansion using <a href="/wiki/Type_Ia_supernova" title="Type Ia supernova">Type Ia supernovae</a> and measurements of temperature fluctuations in the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a>, the time that has passed since that event &#8212; otherwise known as the "<a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">age of the universe</a>" &#8212; is 13.799 &#177; 0.021&#160;billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck_2015_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planck_2015-25">[24]</a></sup> The agreement of independent measurements of this age supports the <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">&#923;CDM model</a> that describes in detail the characteristics of the universe.\n</p><p>Despite being extremely <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">dense</a> at this time&#8212;far denser than is usually required to form a <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a>&#8212;the universe did not re-collapse into a black hole. This may be explained by considering that commonly-used calculations and limits for <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">gravitational collapse</a> are usually based upon objects of relatively constant size, such as <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, and do not apply to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">rapidly expanding space</a> such as the Big Bang.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inflation_and_baryogenesis">Inflation and baryogenesis</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_inflation" title="Cosmic inflation">Cosmic inflation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baryogenesis" title="Baryogenesis">baryogenesis</a></div>\n<p>The earliest phases of the Big Bang are subject to much speculation. In the most common models the universe was filled <a href="/wiki/Homogeneity_and_heterogeneity" title="Homogeneity and heterogeneity">homogeneously</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">isotropically</a> with a very high <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> and huge temperatures and <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressures</a> and was very rapidly expanding and cooling. Approximately 10<sup>&#8722;37</sup> seconds into the expansion, a <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">phase transition</a> caused a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_inflation" title="Cosmic inflation">cosmic inflation</a>, during which the universe grew <a href="/wiki/Exponential_growth" title="Exponential growth">exponentially</a> during which time <a href="/wiki/Quantum_fluctuation" title="Quantum fluctuation">density fluctuations</a> that occurred because of the <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty_principle" title="Uncertainty principle">uncertainty principle</a> were amplified into the seeds that would later form the <a href="/wiki/Structure_formation" title="Structure formation">large-scale structure</a> of the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guth_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-guth-26">[25]</a></sup> After inflation stopped, reheating occurred until the universe obtained the temperatures required for the <a href="/wiki/Pair_production" title="Pair production">production</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Quark%E2%80%93gluon_plasma" title="Quark&#8211;gluon plasma">quark&#8211;gluon plasma</a> as well as all other <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> Temperatures were so high that the random motions of particles were at <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">relativistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_speed" title="Relativistic speed">speeds</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">particle&#8211;antiparticle pairs</a> of all kinds were being continuously created and destroyed in collisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTUW_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-HTUW-7">[7]</a></sup> At some point, an unknown reaction called <a href="/wiki/Baryogenesis" title="Baryogenesis">baryogenesis</a> violated the conservation of <a href="/wiki/Baryon_number" title="Baryon number">baryon number</a>, leading to a very small excess of <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">leptons</a> over antiquarks and antileptons&#8212;of the order of one part in 30&#160;million. This resulted in the predominance of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> over <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a> in the present universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kolb_c6_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-kolb_c6-28">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cooling">Cooling</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">cosmic microwave background radiation</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2MASS_LSS_chart-NEW_Nasa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="627" data-file-width="1239" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/2MASS_LSS_chart-NEW_Nasa.jpg/330px-2MASS_LSS_chart-NEW_Nasa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/2MASS_LSS_chart-NEW_Nasa.jpg/495px-2MASS_LSS_chart-NEW_Nasa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/2MASS_LSS_chart-NEW_Nasa.jpg/660px-2MASS_LSS_chart-NEW_Nasa.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2MASS_LSS_chart-NEW_Nasa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Panoramic view of the entire <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-infrared" title="Near-infrared">near-infrared</a> sky reveals the distribution of galaxies beyond the Milky Way. Galaxies are color-coded by <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The universe continued to decrease in density and fall in temperature, hence the typical energy of each particle was decreasing. <a href="/wiki/Explicit_symmetry_breaking" title="Explicit symmetry breaking">Symmetry breaking</a> phase transitions put the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_force" title="Fundamental force">fundamental forces</a> of physics and the parameters of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elementary_particles" title="Elementary particles">elementary particles</a> into their present form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kolb_c7_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-kolb_c7-29">[28]</a></sup> After about 10<sup>&#8722;11</sup> seconds, the picture becomes less speculative, since particle energies drop to values that can be attained in <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerators</a>. At about 10<sup>&#8722;6</sup> seconds, quarks and gluons combined to form <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryons</a> such as protons and neutrons. The small excess of quarks over antiquarks led to a small excess of baryons over antibaryons. The temperature was now no longer high enough to create new proton&#8211;antiproton pairs (similarly for neutrons&#8211;antineutrons), so a mass annihilation immediately followed, leaving just one in 10<sup>10</sup> of the original protons and neutrons, and none of their antiparticles. A similar process happened at about 1 second for electrons and positrons. After these annihilations, the remaining protons, neutrons and electrons were no longer moving relativistically and the energy density of the universe was dominated by <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> (with a minor contribution from <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a>).\n</p><p>A few minutes into the expansion, when the temperature was about a billion (one thousand million) <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a> and the density was about that of air, neutrons combined with protons to form the universe\'s <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> and helium <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclei</a> in a process called <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kolb_c4_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-kolb_c4-30">[29]</a></sup> Most protons remained uncombined as hydrogen nuclei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peacock_c9_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-peacock_c9-31">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As the universe cooled, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">rest mass</a> energy density of matter came to gravitationally dominate that of the photon <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">radiation</a>. After about 379,000 years, the electrons and nuclei combined into atoms (mostly hydrogen); hence the radiation decoupled from matter and continued through space largely unimpeded. This relic radiation is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">cosmic microwave background radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peacock_c9_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-peacock_c9-31">[30]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">chemistry of life</a> may have begun shortly after the Big Bang, <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">13.8 billion years ago</a>, during a habitable epoch when the universe was only 10&#8211;17 million years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loeb_2014_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loeb_2014-32">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IJA-2014October_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-IJA-2014October-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141202_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141202-34">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_formation">Structure formation</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Structure_formation" title="Structure formation">Structure formation</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WMAP2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/WMAP2.jpg/220px-WMAP2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/WMAP2.jpg/330px-WMAP2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/WMAP2.jpg/440px-WMAP2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WMAP2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s depiction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WMAP" title="WMAP">WMAP</a> satellite gathering data to help scientists understand the Big Bang</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heic1401a-Abell2744-20140107.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4360" data-file-width="3909" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Heic1401a-Abell2744-20140107.jpg/170px-Heic1401a-Abell2744-20140107.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Heic1401a-Abell2744-20140107.jpg/255px-Heic1401a-Abell2744-20140107.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Heic1401a-Abell2744-20140107.jpg/340px-Heic1401a-Abell2744-20140107.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heic1401a-Abell2744-20140107.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Abell_2744" title="Abell 2744">Abell 2744</a> <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_cluster" title="Galaxy cluster">galaxy cluster</a> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#Frontier_Fields_program" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Frontier Fields view</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140107_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140107-35">[34]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Over a long period of time, the slightly denser regions of the nearly uniformly distributed matter gravitationally attracted nearby matter and thus grew even denser, forming gas clouds, stars, galaxies, and the other astronomical structures observable today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTUW_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-HTUW-7">[7]</a></sup> The details of this process depend on the amount and type of matter in the universe. The four possible types of matter are known as <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">cold dark matter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warm_dark_matter" title="Warm dark matter">warm dark matter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hot_dark_matter" title="Hot dark matter">hot dark matter</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baryonic_matter" title="Baryonic matter">baryonic matter</a>. The best measurements available, from <a href="/wiki/Wilkinson_Microwave_Anisotropy_Probe" title="Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe">Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe</a> (WMAP), show that the data is well-fit by a Lambda-CDM model in which dark matter is assumed to be cold (warm dark matter is ruled out by early <a href="/wiki/Reionization" title="Reionization">reionization</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMAP2003Spergel_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-WMAP2003Spergel-36">[35]</a></sup> and is estimated to make up about 23% of the matter/energy of the universe, while baryonic matter makes up about 4.6%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmap7year_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-wmap7year-37">[36]</a></sup> In an "extended model" which includes hot dark matter in the form of <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a>, then if the "physical baryon density" <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Omega _{\\text{b}}h^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#937;<!-- &#937; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtext>b</mtext>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Omega _{\\text{b}}h^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\Omega _{\\text{b}}h^{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a989f9a980520e3bf3b4a76bc93a18ca5fccfe3a" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:5.217ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> is estimated at about 0.023 (this is different from the \'baryon density\' <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Omega _{\\text{b}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#937;<!-- &#937; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtext>b</mtext>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Omega _{\\text{b}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\Omega _{\\text{b}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7407465543b008530fcc4c44cb30880bacd98fd9" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.824ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> expressed as a fraction of the total matter/energy density, which as noted above is about 0.046), and the corresponding cold dark matter density <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Omega _{\\text{c}}h^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#937;<!-- &#937; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtext>c</mtext>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Omega _{\\text{c}}h^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\Omega _{\\text{c}}h^{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c12d26a73654e377b8ebf25465013d9f44ae18cb" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:5.034ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> is about 0.11, the corresponding neutrino density <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Omega _{\\text{v}}h^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#937;<!-- &#937; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtext>v</mtext>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Omega _{\\text{v}}h^{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\Omega _{\\text{v}}h^{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a78dfb3d8853c8ca949417082393a9c9457c5352" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:5.172ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> is estimated to be less than 0.0062.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmap7year_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-wmap7year-37">[36]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmic_acceleration">Cosmic acceleration</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Accelerating_expansion_of_the_universe" title="Accelerating expansion of the universe">Accelerating expansion of the universe</a></div>\n<p>Independent lines of evidence from Type Ia supernovae and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">CMB</a> imply that the universe today is dominated by a mysterious form of energy known as <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>, which apparently permeates all of space. The observations suggest 73% of the total energy density of today\'s universe is in this form. When the universe was very young, it was likely infused with dark energy, but with less space and everything closer together, gravity predominated, and it was slowly braking the expansion. But eventually, after numerous billion years of expansion, the growing abundance of dark energy caused the <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble\'s law">expansion of the universe</a> to slowly begin to accelerate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peebles_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-peebles-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dark energy in its simplest formulation takes the form of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a> term in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einstein%27s_field_equation" title="Einstein\'s field equation">Einstein\'s field equations</a> of general relativity, but its composition and mechanism are unknown and, more generally, the details of its <a href="/wiki/Equation_of_state_(cosmology)" title="Equation of state (cosmology)">equation of state</a> and relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of particle physics continue to be investigated both through observation and theoretically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peebles_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-peebles-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>All of this cosmic evolution after the <a href="/wiki/Inflationary_epoch" title="Inflationary epoch">inflationary epoch</a> can be rigorously described and modeled by the &#923;CDM model of cosmology, which uses the independent frameworks of quantum mechanics and Einstein\'s General Relativity. There is no well-supported model describing the action prior to 10<sup>&#8722;15</sup> seconds or so. Apparently a new unified theory of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">quantum gravitation</a> is needed to break this barrier. Understanding this earliest of eras in the history of the universe is currently one of the greatest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="Unsolved problems in physics">unsolved problems in physics</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Features_of_the_model">Features of the model</span></h2>\n<p>The Big Bang theory depends on two major assumptions: the universality of <a href="/wiki/Physical_law" title="Physical law">physical laws</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_principle" title="Cosmological principle">cosmological principle</a>. The cosmological principle states that on large scales the universe is <a href="/wiki/Homogeneous_space" title="Homogeneous space">homogeneous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isotropy" title="Isotropy">isotropic</a>.\n</p><p>These ideas were initially taken as postulates, but today there are efforts to test each of them. For example, the first assumption has been tested by observations showing that largest possible deviation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fine_structure_constant" title="Fine structure constant">fine structure constant</a> over much of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_universe" title="Age of the universe">age of the universe</a> is of order 10<sup>&#8722;5</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> Also, general relativity has passed stringent <a href="/wiki/Tests_of_general_relativity" title="Tests of general relativity">tests</a> on the scale of the Solar System and binary stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[notes 2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>If the large-scale universe appears isotropic as viewed from Earth, the cosmological principle can be derived from the simpler <a href="/wiki/Copernican_principle" title="Copernican principle">Copernican principle</a>, which states that there is no preferred (or special) observer or vantage point. To this end, the cosmological principle has been confirmed to a level of 10<sup>&#8722;5</sup> via observations of the CMB. The universe has been measured to be homogeneous on the largest scales at the 10% level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_of_space">Expansion of space</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Friedmann%E2%80%93Lema%C3%AEtre%E2%80%93Robertson%E2%80%93Walker_metric" title="Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre&#8211;Robertson&#8211;Walker metric">Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre&#8211;Robertson&#8211;Walker metric</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">Metric expansion of space</a></div>\n<p>General relativity describes spacetime by a <a href="/wiki/Metric_tensor" title="Metric tensor">metric</a>, which determines the distances that separate nearby points. The points, which can be galaxies, stars, or other objects, are themselves specified using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coordinate_chart" title="Coordinate chart">coordinate chart</a> or "grid" that is laid down over all <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a>. The cosmological principle implies that the metric should be <a href="/wiki/Homogeneous_space" title="Homogeneous space">homogeneous</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">isotropic</a> on large scales, which uniquely singles out the <a href="/wiki/Friedmann%E2%80%93Lema%C3%AEtre%E2%80%93Robertson%E2%80%93Walker_metric" title="Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre&#8211;Robertson&#8211;Walker metric">Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre&#8211;Robertson&#8211;Walker metric</a> (FLRW metric). \nThis metric contains a <a href="/wiki/Scale_factor" title="Scale factor">scale factor</a>, which describes how the size of the universe changes with time. This enables a convenient choice of a <a href="/wiki/Coordinate_system" title="Coordinate system">coordinate system</a> to be made, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comoving_coordinates" title="Comoving coordinates">comoving coordinates</a>. In this coordinate system, the grid expands along with the universe, and objects that are moving only because of the expansion of the universe, remain at fixed points on the grid. While their <i>coordinate</i> distance (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comoving_distance" title="Comoving distance">comoving distance</a>) remains constant, the <i>physical</i> distance between two such co-moving points expands proportionally with the <a href="/wiki/Scale_factor" title="Scale factor">scale factor</a> of the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Big Bang is not an explosion of matter moving outward to fill an empty universe. Instead, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">space itself expands</a> with time everywhere and increases the physical distance between two comoving points. In other words, the Big Bang is not an explosion <i>in space</i>, but rather an expansion <i>of space</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTUW_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-HTUW-7">[7]</a></sup> Because the FLRW metric assumes a uniform distribution of mass and energy, it applies to our universe only on large scales&#8212;local concentrations of matter such as our galaxy are gravitationally bound and as such do not experience the large-scale expansion of space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Horizons">Horizons</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_cosmological_horizons" title="List of cosmological horizons">List of cosmological horizons</a></div>\n<p>An important feature of the Big Bang spacetime is the presence of <a href="/wiki/Particle_horizon" title="Particle horizon">particle horizons</a>. Since the universe has a finite age, and light travels at a finite speed, there may be events in the past whose light has not had time to reach us. This places a limit or a <i>past horizon</i> on the most distant objects that can be observed. Conversely, because space is expanding, and more distant objects are receding ever more quickly, light emitted by us today may never "catch up" to very distant objects. This defines a <i>future horizon</i>, which limits the events in the future that we will be able to influence. The presence of either type of horizon depends on the details of the <a href="/wiki/Friedmann%E2%80%93Lema%C3%AEtre%E2%80%93Robertson%E2%80%93Walker_metric" title="Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre&#8211;Robertson&#8211;Walker metric">FLRW</a> model that describes our universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kolb_c3_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-kolb_c3-43">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Our understanding of the universe back to very early times <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang#Horizon_problem" title="Big Bang">suggests</a> that there is a past horizon, though in practice our view is also limited by the opacity of the universe at early times. So our view cannot extend further backward in time, though the horizon recedes in space. If the expansion of the universe continues to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accelerating_universe" title="Accelerating universe">accelerate</a>, there is a future horizon as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kolb_c3_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-kolb_c3-43">[41]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Big_Bang_theory" title="History of the Big Bang theory">History of the Big Bang theory</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_cosmological_theories" title="Timeline of cosmological theories">Timeline of cosmological theories</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>\n<p>English astronomer <a href="/wiki/Fred_Hoyle" title="Fred Hoyle">Fred Hoyle</a> is credited with coining the term "Big Bang" during a 1949 BBC radio broadcast, saying: "These theories were based on the hypothesis that all the matter in the universe was created in one big bang at a particular time in the remote past."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is popularly reported that Hoyle, who favored an alternative "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steady_state_universe" title="Steady state universe">steady state</a>" cosmological model, intended this to be pejorative,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup> but Hoyle explicitly denied this and said it was just a striking image meant to highlight the difference between the two models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitton_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mitton-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>129</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h3>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent"><i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Ultra-Deep_Field#Hubble_eXtreme_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Ultra-Deep Field">Hubble eXtreme Deep Field (XDF)</a></i></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:XDF-scale.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2489" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/XDF-scale.jpg/400px-XDF-scale.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Ultra-Deep_Field#Hubble_eXtreme_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Ultra-Deep Field">XDF</a></i> size compared to the size of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> &#8211; several thousand <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a>, each consisting of billions of <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, are in this small view.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Constellation_Fornax,_EXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2078" data-file-width="2382" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Constellation_Fornax%2C_EXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg/200px-Constellation_Fornax%2C_EXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Constellation_Fornax%2C_EXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg/300px-Constellation_Fornax%2C_EXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Constellation_Fornax%2C_EXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg/400px-Constellation_Fornax%2C_EXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Ultra-Deep_Field#Hubble_eXtreme_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Ultra-Deep Field">XDF</a></i> (2012) view &#8211; each light speck is a galaxy &#8211; some of these are as old as 13.2 billion years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20120925_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20120925-49">[47]</a></sup> &#8211; the universe is estimated to contain 200 billion galaxies.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:XDF-separated.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2101" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/XDF-separated.jpg/200px-XDF-separated.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/XDF-separated.jpg/300px-XDF-separated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/XDF-separated.jpg/400px-XDF-separated.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Ultra-Deep_Field#Hubble_eXtreme_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Ultra-Deep Field">XDF</a></i> image shows fully mature galaxies in the foreground plane &#8211; nearly mature galaxies from 5 to 9 billion years ago &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Protogalaxy" title="Protogalaxy">protogalaxies</a>, blazing with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Young_star" title="Young star">young stars</a>, beyond 9 billion years.</div></div></div></div>\n<p>The Big Bang theory developed from observations of the structure of the universe and from theoretical considerations. In 1912 <a href="/wiki/Vesto_Slipher" title="Vesto Slipher">Vesto Slipher</a> measured the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" title="Doppler shift">Doppler shift</a> of a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiral_nebula" title="Spiral nebula">spiral nebula</a>" (spiral nebula is the obsolete term for spiral galaxies), and soon discovered that almost all such nebulae were receding from Earth. He did not grasp the cosmological implications of this fact, and indeed at the time it was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shapley%E2%80%93Curtis_debate" title="Shapley&#8211;Curtis debate">highly controversial</a> whether or not these nebulae were "island universes" outside our <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup> Ten years later, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Friedmann" title="Alexander Friedmann">Alexander Friedmann</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">cosmologist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">mathematician</a>, derived the <a href="/wiki/Friedmann_equations" title="Friedmann equations">Friedmann equations</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einstein_equation" title="Einstein equation">Albert Einstein\'s equations of general relativity</a>, showing that the universe might be expanding in contrast to the <a href="/wiki/Static_universe" title="Static universe">static universe</a> model advocated by Einstein at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-af1922_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-af1922-52">[50]</a></sup> In 1924 Edwin Hubble\'s measurement of the great distance to the nearest spiral nebulae showed that these systems were indeed other galaxies. Independently deriving Friedmann\'s equations in 1927, <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lema&#238;tre">Georges Lema&#238;tre</a>, a Belgian physicist, proposed that the inferred recession of the nebulae was due to the expansion of the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gl1927_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-gl1927-53">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1931 Lema&#238;tre went further and suggested that the evident expansion of the universe, if projected back in time, meant that the further in the past the smaller the universe was, until at some finite time in the past all the mass of the universe was concentrated into a single point, a "primeval atom" where and when the fabric of time and space came into existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Starting in 1924, Hubble painstakingly developed a series of distance indicators, the forerunner of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_distance_ladder" title="Cosmic distance ladder">cosmic distance ladder</a>, using the 100-inch (2.5&#160;m) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hooker_telescope" title="Hooker telescope">Hooker telescope</a> at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Wilson_Observatory" title="Mount Wilson Observatory">Mount Wilson Observatory</a>. This allowed him to estimate distances to galaxies whose redshifts had already been measured, mostly by Slipher. In 1929 Hubble discovered a correlation between distance and recession velocity&#8212;now known as <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble\'s law">Hubble\'s law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubble_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-hubble-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-christianson_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-christianson-55">[53]</a></sup> Lema&#238;tre had already shown that this was expected, given the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_principle" title="Cosmological principle">cosmological principle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peebles_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-peebles-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nIn the 1920s and 1930s almost every major cosmologist preferred an eternal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steady_state_theory" title="Steady state theory">steady state</a> universe, and several complained that the beginning of time implied by the Big Bang imported religious concepts into physics; this objection was later repeated by supporters of the steady state theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup> This perception was enhanced by the fact that the originator of the Big Bang theory, <a href="/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre" title="Georges Lema&#238;tre">Georges Lema&#238;tre</a>, was a Roman Catholic priest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Eddington" title="Arthur Eddington">Arthur Eddington</a> agreed with <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> that the universe did not have a beginning in time, <i>viz</i>., that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arguments_for_eternity#Argument_from_the_nature_of_matter" title="Arguments for eternity">matter is eternal</a>. A beginning in time was "repugnant" to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> Lema&#238;tre, however, thought that</p><blockquote><p>If the world has begun with a single <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum</a>, the notions of space and time would altogether fail to have any meaning at the beginning; they would only begin to have a sensible meaning when the original quantum had been divided into a sufficient number of quanta. If this suggestion is correct, the beginning of the world happened a little before the beginning of space and time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>During the 1930s other ideas were proposed as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-standard_cosmologies" title="Non-standard cosmologies">non-standard cosmologies</a> to explain Hubble\'s observations, including the <a href="/wiki/Milne_model" title="Milne model">Milne model</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oscillatory_universe" title="Oscillatory universe">oscillatory universe</a> (originally suggested by Friedmann, but advocated by Albert Einstein and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_Tolman" title="Richard Tolman">Richard Tolman</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Zwicky" title="Fritz Zwicky">Fritz Zwicky</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Tired_light" title="Tired light">tired light</a> hypothesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, two distinct possibilities emerged. One was Fred Hoyle\'s <a href="/wiki/Steady_state_model" title="Steady state model">steady state model</a>, whereby new matter would be created as the universe seemed to expand. In this model the universe is roughly the same at any point in time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup> The other was Lema&#238;tre\'s Big Bang theory, advocated and developed by <a href="/wiki/George_Gamow" title="George Gamow">George Gamow</a>, who introduced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big bang nucleosynthesis">big bang nucleosynthesis</a> (BBN)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup> and whose associates, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ralph_Alpher" title="Ralph Alpher">Ralph Alpher</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Herman" title="Robert Herman">Robert Herman</a>, predicted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">CMB</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup> Ironically, it was Hoyle who coined the phrase that came to be applied to Lema&#238;tre\'s theory, referring to it as "this <i>big bang</i> idea" during a <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio" title="BBC Radio">BBC Radio</a> broadcast in March 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitton_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mitton-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[notes 3]</a></sup> For a while, support was split between these two theories. Eventually, the observational evidence, most notably from radio <a href="/wiki/Source_counts" title="Source counts">source counts</a>, began to favor Big Bang over Steady State. The discovery and confirmation of the CMB in 1964 secured the Big Bang as the best theory of the origin and evolution of the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-penzias_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-penzias-69">[66]</a></sup> Much of the current work in cosmology includes understanding how galaxies form in the context of the Big Bang, understanding the physics of the universe at earlier and earlier times, and reconciling observations with the basic theory.\n</p><p>In 1968 and 1970 <a href="/wiki/Roger_Penrose" title="Roger Penrose">Roger Penrose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a>, and <a href="/wiki/George_F._R._Ellis" title="George F. R. Ellis">George F. R. Ellis</a> published papers where they showed that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematical_singularities" title="Mathematical singularities">mathematical singularities</a> were an inevitable initial condition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_Relativity" title="General Relativity">general relativistic</a> models of the Big Bang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup> Then, from the 1970s to the 1990s, cosmologists worked on characterizing the features of the Big Bang universe and resolving outstanding problems. In 1981, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Guth" title="Alan Guth">Alan Guth</a> made a breakthrough in theoretical work on resolving certain outstanding theoretical <a href="#Problems">problems in the Big Bang theory</a> with the introduction of an epoch of rapid expansion in the early universe he called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_inflation" title="Cosmic inflation">inflation</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup> Meanwhile, during these decades, two questions in <a href="/wiki/Observational_cosmology" title="Observational cosmology">observational cosmology</a> that generated much discussion and disagreement were over the precise values of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_Constant" title="Hubble Constant">Hubble Constant</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup> and the matter-density of the universe (before the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>, thought to be the key predictor for the eventual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fate_of_the_universe" title="Fate of the universe">fate of the universe</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the mid-1990s, observations of certain <a href="/wiki/Globular_cluster" title="Globular cluster">globular clusters</a> appeared to indicate that they were about 15&#160;billion years old, which <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_age_problem" title="Cosmic age problem">conflicted</a> with most then-current estimates of the age of the universe (and indeed with the age measured today). This issue was later resolved when new computer simulations, which included the effects of mass loss due to <a href="/wiki/Stellar_wind" title="Stellar wind">stellar winds</a>, indicated a much younger age for globular clusters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> While there still remain some questions as to how accurately the ages of the clusters are measured, globular clusters are of interest to cosmology as some of the oldest objects in the universe.\n</p><p>Significant progress in Big Bang cosmology has been made since the late 1990s as a result of advances in <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a> technology as well as the analysis of data from satellites such as <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_Background_Explorer" title="Cosmic Background Explorer">COBE</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cobe_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-cobe-76">[73]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WMAP" title="WMAP">WMAP</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmap1year_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-wmap1year-77">[74]</a></sup> Cosmologists now have fairly precise and accurate measurements of many of the parameters of the Big Bang model, and have made the unexpected discovery that the expansion of the universe appears to be accelerating.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observational_evidence">Observational evidence</span></h2>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:27%; ; padding:8px;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" &#8220; ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" &#8221; ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">"[The] big bang picture is too firmly grounded in data from every area to be proved invalid in its general features."</div>\n<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""><a href="/wiki/Lawrence_M._Krauss" title="Lawrence M. Krauss">Lawrence Krauss</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>The earliest and most direct observational evidence of the validity of the theory are the expansion of the universe according to <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble\'s law">Hubble\'s law</a> (as indicated by the redshifts of galaxies), discovery and measurement of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a> and the relative abundances of light elements produced by <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a>. More recent evidence includes observations of <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation_and_evolution" title="Galaxy formation and evolution">galaxy formation and evolution</a>, and the distribution of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_cosmos" title="Large-scale structure of the cosmos">large-scale cosmic structures</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup> These are sometimes called the "four pillars" of the Big Bang theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Precise modern models of the Big Bang appeal to various exotic physical phenomena that have not been observed in terrestrial laboratory experiments or incorporated into the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a>. Of these features, <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> is currently subjected to the most active laboratory investigations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup> Remaining issues include the <a href="/wiki/Cuspy_halo_problem" title="Cuspy halo problem">cuspy halo problem</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_galaxy_problem" title="Dwarf galaxy problem">dwarf galaxy problem</a> of cold dark matter. Dark energy is also an area of intense interest for scientists, but it is not clear whether direct detection of dark energy will be possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Inflation_(cosmology)" title="Inflation (cosmology)">Inflation</a> and baryogenesis remain more speculative features of current Big Bang models. Viable, quantitative explanations for such phenomena are still being sought. These are currently <a href="/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="List of unsolved problems in physics">unsolved problems in physics</a>.\n</p><p><span id="Hubble\'s_law_expansion"></span>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Hubble.27s_law_and_the_expansion_of_space"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hubble\'s_law_and_the_expansion_of_space">Hubble\'s law and the expansion of space</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble\'s law">Hubble\'s law</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">Metric expansion of space</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Distance_measures_(cosmology)" title="Distance measures (cosmology)">Distance measures (cosmology)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scale_factor_(universe)" title="Scale factor (universe)">Scale factor (universe)</a></div>\n<p>Observations of distant galaxies and <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">quasars</a> show that these objects are redshifted&#8212;the <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> emitted from them has been shifted to longer wavelengths. This can be seen by taking a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frequency_spectrum" title="Frequency spectrum">frequency spectrum</a> of an object and matching the <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopic</a> pattern of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emission_line" title="Emission line">emission lines</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absorption_line" title="Absorption line">absorption lines</a> corresponding to <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> interacting with the light. These redshifts are <a href="/wiki/Homogeneity_(physics)" title="Homogeneity (physics)">uniformly</a> isotropic, distributed evenly among the observed objects in all directions. If the redshift is interpreted as a Doppler shift, the recessional <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> of the object can be calculated. For some galaxies, it is possible to estimate distances via the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_distance_ladder" title="Cosmic distance ladder">cosmic distance ladder</a>. When the recessional velocities are plotted against these distances, a linear relationship known as Hubble\'s law is observed:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubble_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-hubble-20">[20]</a></sup>\n<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v=H_{0}D}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>D</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v=H_{0}D}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle v=H_{0}D}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f54dd8bb79c20c2f3240c51cee81c418ea0f9d83" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex;
