width:9.136ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>\nwhere\n</p>\n<ul><li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>v</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="v" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e07b00e7fc0847fbd16391c778d65bc25c452597" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.128ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is the recessional <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a> or other distant object,</li>\n<li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle D}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>D</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle D}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="D" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f34a0c600395e5d4345287e21fb26efd386990e6" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.924ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comoving_distance" title="Comoving distance">comoving distance</a> to the object, and</li>\n<li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle H_{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle H_{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="H_{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/43910602a221b7a4c373791f94793e3008622070" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.985ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_constant" title="Hubble\'s constant">Hubble\'s constant</a>, measured to be <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001704000000000000&#9824;</span>70.4<span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right;">+1.3<br/>&#8722;1.4</span></span></span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilometres" title="Kilometres">km</a>/<a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">s</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megaparsec" title="Megaparsec">Mpc</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WMAP" title="WMAP">WMAP</a> probe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmap7year_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-wmap7year-37">[36]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble\'s law">Hubble\'s law</a> has two possible explanations. Either we are at the center of an explosion of galaxies&#8212;which is untenable given the <a href="/wiki/Copernican_principle" title="Copernican principle">Copernican principle</a>&#8212;or the universe is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">uniformly expanding</a> everywhere. This universal expansion was predicted from general relativity by Alexander Friedmann in 1922<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-af1922_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-af1922-52">[50]</a></sup> and Georges Lema&#238;tre in 1927,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gl1927_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-gl1927-53">[51]</a></sup> well before Hubble made his 1929 analysis and observations, and it remains the cornerstone of the Big Bang theory as developed by <a href="/wiki/Friedmann%E2%80%93Lema%C3%AEtre%E2%80%93Robertson%E2%80%93Walker_metric" title="Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre&#8211;Robertson&#8211;Walker metric">Friedmann, Lema&#238;tre, Robertson, and Walker</a>.\n</p><p>The theory requires the relation <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v=HD}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mi>D</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v=HD}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle v=HD}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cf4c255ecc475a7ce9c30bbc9dde534d48f88892" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:8.214ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> to hold at all times, where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle D}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>D</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle D}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="D" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f34a0c600395e5d4345287e21fb26efd386990e6" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.924ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comoving_distance" title="Comoving distance">comoving distance</a>, <i>v</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Recessional_velocity" title="Recessional velocity">recessional velocity</a>, and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>v</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="v" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e07b00e7fc0847fbd16391c778d65bc25c452597" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.128ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span>, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle H}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>H</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle H}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="H" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/75a9edddcca2f782014371f75dca39d7e13a9c1b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.064ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle D}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>D</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle D}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="D" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f34a0c600395e5d4345287e21fb26efd386990e6" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.924ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> vary as the universe expands (hence we write <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle H_{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>H</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle H_{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="H_{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/43910602a221b7a4c373791f94793e3008622070" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.985ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> to denote the present-day Hubble "constant"). For distances much smaller than the size of the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>, the Hubble redshift can be thought of as the Doppler shift corresponding to the recession velocity <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>v</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="v" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e07b00e7fc0847fbd16391c778d65bc25c452597" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.128ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span>. However, the redshift is not a true Doppler shift, but rather the result of the expansion of the universe between the time the light was emitted and the time that it was detected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peacock_c3_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-peacock_c3-83">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>That <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">space is undergoing metric expansion</a> is shown by direct observational evidence of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_principle" title="Cosmological principle">Cosmological principle</a> and the Copernican principle, which together with Hubble\'s law have no other explanation. Astronomical redshifts are extremely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">isotropic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Homogeneity_(physics)#Translation_invariance" title="Homogeneity (physics)">homogeneous</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubble_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-hubble-20">[20]</a></sup> supporting the Cosmological principle that the universe looks the same in all directions, along with much other evidence. If the redshifts were the result of an explosion from a center distant from us, they would not be so similar in different directions.\n</p><p>Measurements of the effects of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">cosmic microwave background radiation</a> on the dynamics of distant astrophysical systems in 2000 proved the Copernican principle, that, on a cosmological scale, the Earth is not in a central position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> Radiation from the Big Bang was demonstrably warmer at earlier times throughout the universe. Uniform cooling of the CMB over billions of years is explainable only if the universe is experiencing a metric expansion, and excludes the possibility that we are near the unique center of an explosion.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation">Cosmic microwave background radiation</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">Cosmic microwave background radiation</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cmbr.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Cmbr.svg/220px-Cmbr.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Cmbr.svg/330px-Cmbr.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Cmbr.svg/440px-Cmbr.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cmbr.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The cosmic microwave background spectrum measured by the FIRAS instrument on the COBE satellite is the most-precisely measured black body spectrum in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dpf99_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-dpf99-85">[82]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_point" title="Data point">data points</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_error_of_estimation" title="Standard error of estimation">error bars</a> on this graph are obscured by the theoretical curve.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1964 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arno_Penzias" title="Arno Penzias">Arno Penzias</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Woodrow_Wilson" title="Robert Woodrow Wilson">Robert Wilson</a> serendipitously discovered the cosmic background radiation, an omnidirectional signal in the <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> band.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-penzias_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-penzias-69">[66]</a></sup> Their discovery provided substantial confirmation of the big-bang predictions by Alpher, Herman and Gamow around 1950. Through the 1970s the radiation was found to be approximately consistent with a black body spectrum in all directions; this spectrum has been redshifted by the expansion of the universe, and today corresponds to approximately 2.725&#160;K. This tipped the balance of evidence in favor of the Big Bang model, and Penzias and Wilson were awarded a <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> in 1978.\n</p><p>The <i>surface of last scattering</i> corresponding to emission of the CMB occurs shortly after <i><a href="/wiki/Recombination_(cosmology)" title="Recombination (cosmology)">recombination</a></i>, the epoch when neutral hydrogen becomes stable. Prior to this, the universe comprised a hot dense photon-baryon plasma sea where photons were quickly <a href="/wiki/Thomson_scattering" title="Thomson scattering">scattered</a> from free charged particles. Peaking at around <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7013117394272000000&#9824;</span>372<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>14&#160;kyr</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMAP2003Spergel_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-WMAP2003Spergel-36">[35]</a></sup> the mean free path for a photon becomes long enough to reach the present day and the universe becomes transparent.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="4096" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png/220px-Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png/330px-Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png/440px-Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ilc_9yr_moll4096.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>9 year <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WMAP" title="WMAP">WMAP</a> image of the cosmic microwave background radiation (2012).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arXiv-20121220_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-arXiv-20121220-86">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20121221_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20121221-87">[84]</a></sup> The radiation is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">isotropic</a> to roughly one part in 100,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1989, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> launched the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_Background_Explorer_satellite" title="Cosmic Background Explorer satellite">Cosmic Background Explorer satellite</a> (COBE), which made two major advances: in 1990, high-precision spectrum measurements showed that the <abbr title="cosmic microwave background raditation">CMB</abbr> frequency spectrum is an almost perfect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blackbody" title="Blackbody">blackbody</a> with no deviations at a level of 1 part in 10<sup>4</sup>, and measured a residual temperature of 2.726&#160;K (more recent measurements have revised this figure down slightly to 2.7255&#160;K); then in 1992, further COBE measurements discovered tiny fluctuations (anisotropies) in the CMB temperature across the sky, at a level of about one part in 10<sup>5</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cobe_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-cobe-76">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_C._Mather" title="John C. Mather">John C. Mather</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Smoot" title="George Smoot">George Smoot</a> were awarded the 2006 Nobel Prize in Physics for their leadership in these results.\n</p><p>During the following decade, CMB anisotropies were further investigated by a large number of ground-based and balloon experiments. In 2000&#8211;2001 several experiments, most notably <a href="/wiki/BOOMERanG_experiment" title="BOOMERanG experiment">BOOMERanG</a>, found the <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe" title="Shape of the universe">shape of the universe</a> to be spatially almost flat by measuring the typical angular size (the size on the sky) of the anisotropies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In early 2003, the first results of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WMAP" title="WMAP">Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe</a> (WMAP) were released, yielding what were at the time the most accurate values for some of the cosmological parameters. The results disproved several specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_inflation" title="Cosmic inflation">cosmic inflation</a> models, but are consistent with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inflation_theory" title="Inflation theory">inflation theory</a> in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmap1year_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-wmap1year-77">[74]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Planck_(spacecraft)" title="Planck (spacecraft)">Planck</a> space probe was launched in May 2009. Other ground and balloon based <a href="/wiki/List_of_cosmic_microwave_background_experiments" title="List of cosmic microwave background experiments">cosmic microwave background experiments</a> are ongoing.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abundance_of_primordial_elements">Abundance of primordial elements</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a></div>\n<p>Using the Big Bang model it is possible to calculate the concentration of <a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4">helium-4</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3">helium-3</a>, deuterium, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithium-7" title="Lithium-7">lithium-7</a> in the universe as ratios to the amount of ordinary hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kolb_c4_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-kolb_c4-30">[29]</a></sup> The relative abundances depend on a single parameter, the ratio of <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> to baryons. This value can be calculated independently from the detailed structure of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">CMB</a> fluctuations. The ratios predicted (by mass, not by number) are about 0.25 for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^4He/H}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mmultiscripts>\n<mtext>He</mtext>\n<none></none>\n<none></none>\n<mprescripts></mprescripts>\n<none></none>\n<mn>4</mn>\n</mmultiscripts>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mtext>H</mtext>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^4He/H}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^4He/H}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0fa21196a64cc2a789cfda6e2931aea1100c8c0c" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.677ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span>, about 10<sup>&#8722;3</sup> for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^2H/H}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mmultiscripts>\n<mtext>H</mtext>\n<none></none>\n<none></none>\n<mprescripts></mprescripts>\n<none></none>\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mmultiscripts>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mtext>H</mtext>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^2H/H}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^2H/H}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/01ca7d2b4f3071eec57d0e9576d07834bdacfe23" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:5.645ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span>, about 10<sup>&#8722;4</sup> for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^3He/H}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mmultiscripts>\n<mtext>He</mtext>\n<none></none>\n<none></none>\n<mprescripts></mprescripts>\n<none></none>\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mmultiscripts>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mtext>H</mtext>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^3He/H}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^3He/H}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f561617839ded259c6749427f19d3b8aef9f76a6" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.677ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span> and about 10<sup>&#8722;9</sup> for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^7Li/H}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mmultiscripts>\n<mtext>Li</mtext>\n<none></none>\n<none></none>\n<mprescripts></mprescripts>\n<none></none>\n<mn>7</mn>\n</mmultiscripts>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mtext>H</mtext>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^7Li/H}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^7Li/H}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/812f298e4cbc2968fbcfa8c30cdab47f6e302425" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.001ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kolb_c4_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-kolb_c4-30">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The measured abundances all agree at least roughly with those predicted from a single value of the baryon-to-photon ratio. The agreement is excellent for deuterium, close but formally discrepant for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^4He}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mmultiscripts>\n<mtext>He</mtext>\n<none></none>\n<none></none>\n<mprescripts></mprescripts>\n<none></none>\n<mn>4</mn>\n</mmultiscripts>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^4He}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^4He}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/124e8eb25cabfc41d754bd6c3adf2ab2c489d3b9" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.772ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span>, and off by a factor of two for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^7Li}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mmultiscripts>\n<mtext>Li</mtext>\n<none></none>\n<none></none>\n<mprescripts></mprescripts>\n<none></none>\n<mn>7</mn>\n</mmultiscripts>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^7Li}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^7Li}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/052ade11a4b15ff9d65a7e2cd8c9406fc76780f7" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.096ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span>; in the latter two cases there are substantial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systematic_error" title="Systematic error">systematic uncertainties</a>. Nonetheless, the general consistency with abundances predicted by Big Bang nucleosynthesis is strong evidence for the Big Bang, as the theory is the only known explanation for the relative abundances of light elements, and it is virtually impossible to "tune" the Big Bang to produce much more or less than 20&#8211;30% helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup> Indeed, there is no obvious reason outside of the Big Bang that, for example, the young universe (i.e., before star formation, as determined by studying matter supposedly free of <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a> products) should have more helium than deuterium or more deuterium than <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^3He}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mmultiscripts>\n<mtext>He</mtext>\n<none></none>\n<none></none>\n<mprescripts></mprescripts>\n<none></none>\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mmultiscripts>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^3He}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\ce {^3He}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b442e489c727ad7f9d146775818a46abc71147a5" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.772ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span>, and in constant ratios, too.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryden2003_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ryden2003-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>182&#8211;185</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Galactic_evolution_and_distribution">Galactic evolution and distribution</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation_and_evolution" title="Galaxy formation and evolution">Galaxy formation and evolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Structure_formation" title="Structure formation">Structure formation</a></div>\n<p>Detailed observations of the <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_morphological_classification" title="Galaxy morphological classification">morphology</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_cosmos" title="Large-scale structure of the cosmos">distribution</a> of galaxies and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quasars" title="Quasars">quasars</a> are in agreement with the current state of the Big Bang theory. A combination of observations and theory suggest that the first quasars and galaxies formed about a billion years after the Big Bang, and since then, larger structures have been forming, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galaxy_clusters" title="Galaxy clusters">galaxy clusters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Supercluster" title="Supercluster">superclusters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertschinger_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bertschinger-94">[91]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Populations of stars have been aging and evolving, so that distant galaxies (which are observed as they were in the early universe) appear very different from nearby galaxies (observed in a more recent state). Moreover, galaxies that formed relatively recently, appear markedly different from galaxies formed at similar distances but shortly after the Big Bang. These observations are strong arguments against the steady-state model. Observations of <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">star formation</a>, galaxy and quasar distributions and larger structures, agree well with Big Bang simulations of the formation of structure in the universe, and are helping to complete details of the theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertschinger_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bertschinger-94">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primordial_gas_clouds">Primordial gas clouds</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA17993-DetectorsForInfantUniverseStudies-20140317.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2884" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/PIA17993-DetectorsForInfantUniverseStudies-20140317.jpg/220px-PIA17993-DetectorsForInfantUniverseStudies-20140317.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/PIA17993-DetectorsForInfantUniverseStudies-20140317.jpg/330px-PIA17993-DetectorsForInfantUniverseStudies-20140317.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/PIA17993-DetectorsForInfantUniverseStudies-20140317.jpg/440px-PIA17993-DetectorsForInfantUniverseStudies-20140317.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA17993-DetectorsForInfantUniverseStudies-20140317.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Focal_plane" title="Focal plane">Focal plane</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BICEP2" title="BICEP2">BICEP2 telescope</a> under a microscope - used to search for polarization in the CMB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BICEP2-2014_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-BICEP2-2014-96">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140317_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140317-97">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20140317_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20140317-98">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20140324_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20140324-99">[96]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In 2011, astronomers found what they believe to be pristine clouds of primordial gas by analyzing absorption lines in the spectra of distant quasars. Before this discovery, all other astronomical objects have been observed to contain heavy elements that are formed in stars. These two clouds of gas contain no elements heavier than hydrogen and deuterium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup> Since the clouds of gas have no heavy elements, they likely formed in the first few minutes after the Big Bang, during <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_lines_of_evidence">Other lines of evidence</span></h3>\n<p>The age of the universe as estimated from the Hubble expansion and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">CMB</a> is now in good agreement with other estimates using the ages of the oldest stars, both as measured by applying the theory of <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">stellar evolution</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Globular_clusters" title="Globular clusters">globular clusters</a> and through <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radiometric dating</a> of individual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_II" title="Population II">Population II</a> stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The prediction that the CMB temperature was higher in the past has been experimentally supported by observations of very low temperature absorption lines in gas clouds at high redshift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup> This prediction also implies that the amplitude of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunyaev%E2%80%93Zel%27dovich_effect" title="Sunyaev&#8211;Zel\'dovich effect">Sunyaev&#8211;Zel\'dovich effect</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clusters_of_galaxies" title="Clusters of galaxies">clusters of galaxies</a> does not depend directly on redshift. Observations have found this to be roughly true, but this effect depends on cluster properties that do change with cosmic time, making precise measurements difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_observations">Future observations</span></h3>\n<p>Future <a href="/wiki/Gravitational-wave_observatory" title="Gravitational-wave observatory">gravitational waves observatories</a> might be able to detect primordial gravitational waves, relics of the early universe, up to less than a second after the Big Bang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ghosh_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ghosh-106">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billings_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Billings-107">[104]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Problems_and_related_issues_in_physics"><span id="Problems"></span>Problems and related issues in physics</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="List of unsolved problems in physics">List of unsolved problems in physics</a></div>\n<p>As with any theory, a number of mysteries and problems have arisen as a result of the development of the Big Bang theory. Some of these mysteries and problems have been resolved while others are still outstanding. Proposed solutions to some of the problems in the Big Bang model have revealed new mysteries of their own. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Horizon_problem" title="Horizon problem">horizon problem</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_monopole_problem" title="Magnetic monopole problem">magnetic monopole problem</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Flatness_problem" title="Flatness problem">flatness problem</a> are most commonly resolved with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_inflation" title="Cosmic inflation">inflationary theory</a>, but the details of the inflationary universe are still left unresolved and many, including some founders of the theory, say it has been disproven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[108]</a></sup> What follows are a list of the mysterious aspects of the Big Bang theory still under intense investigation by cosmologists and astrophysicists.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baryon_asymmetry">Baryon asymmetry</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baryon_asymmetry" title="Baryon asymmetry">Baryon asymmetry</a></div>\n<p>It is not yet understood why the universe has more matter than antimatter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-112">[109]</a></sup> It is generally assumed that when the universe was young and very hot it was in statistical equilibrium and contained equal numbers of baryons and antibaryons. However, observations suggest that the universe, including its most distant parts, is made almost entirely of matter. A process called baryogenesis was hypothesized to account for the asymmetry. For baryogenesis to occur, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sakharov_conditions" title="Sakharov conditions">Sakharov conditions</a> must be satisfied. These require that baryon number is not conserved, that <a href="/wiki/C-symmetry" title="C-symmetry">C-symmetry</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CP-symmetry" title="CP-symmetry">CP-symmetry</a> are violated and that the universe depart from <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_equilibrium" title="Thermodynamic equilibrium">thermodynamic equilibrium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sakharov_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-sakharov-113">[110]</a></sup> All these conditions occur in the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a>, but the effects are not strong enough to explain the present baryon asymmetry.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dark_energy">Dark energy</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">Dark energy</a></div>\n<p>Measurements of the <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a>&#8211;<a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">magnitude</a> relation for <a href="/wiki/Type_Ia_supernova" title="Type Ia supernova">type Ia supernovae</a> indicate that the expansion of the universe has been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accelerating_universe" title="Accelerating universe">accelerating</a> since the universe was about half its present age. To explain this acceleration, general relativity requires that much of the energy in the universe consists of a component with large <a href="/wiki/Equation_of_state_(cosmology)" title="Equation of state (cosmology)">negative pressure</a>, dubbed "dark energy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peebles_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-peebles-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dark energy, though speculative, solves numerous problems. Measurements of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">cosmic microwave background</a> indicate that the universe is very nearly spatially flat, and therefore according to general relativity the universe must have almost exactly the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_density_(cosmology)" title="Critical density (cosmology)">critical density</a> of mass/energy. But the mass density of the universe can be measured from its gravitational clustering, and is found to have only about 30% of the critical density.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peebles_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-peebles-13">[13]</a></sup> Since theory suggests that dark energy does not cluster in the usual way it is the best explanation for the "missing" energy density. Dark energy also helps to explain two geometrical measures of the overall curvature of the universe, one using the frequency of <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lens" title="Gravitational lens">gravitational lenses</a>, and the other using the characteristic pattern of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_universe" title="Large-scale structure of the universe">large-scale structure</a> as a cosmic ruler.\n</p><p>Negative pressure is believed to be a property of <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_energy" title="Vacuum energy">vacuum energy</a>, but the exact nature and existence of dark energy remains one of the great mysteries of the Big Bang. Results from the WMAP team in 2008 are in accordance with a universe that consists of 73% dark energy, 23% dark matter, 4.6% regular matter and less than 1% neutrinos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wmap7year_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-wmap7year-37">[36]</a></sup> According to theory, the energy density in matter decreases with the expansion of the universe, but the dark energy density remains constant (or nearly so) as the universe expands. Therefore, matter made up a larger fraction of the total energy of the universe in the past than it does today, but its fractional contribution will fall in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Far_future" title="Far future">far future</a> as dark energy becomes even more dominant.\n</p><p>The dark energy component of the universe has been explained by theorists using a variety of competing theories including Einstein\'s <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a> but also extending to more exotic forms of <a href="/wiki/Quintessence_(physics)" title="Quintessence (physics)">quintessence</a> or other modified gravity schemes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant_problem" title="Cosmological constant problem">cosmological constant problem</a>, sometimes called the "most embarrassing problem in physics", results from the apparent discrepancy between the measured energy density of dark energy, and the one naively predicted from <a href="/wiki/Planck_units" title="Planck units">Planck units</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[112]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dark_matter">Dark matter</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">Dark matter</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cosmological_Composition_%E2%80%93_Pie_Chart.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="298" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Cosmological_Composition_%E2%80%93_Pie_Chart.svg/330px-Cosmological_Composition_%E2%80%93_Pie_Chart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Cosmological_Composition_%E2%80%93_Pie_Chart.svg/495px-Cosmological_Composition_%E2%80%93_Pie_Chart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Cosmological_Composition_%E2%80%93_Pie_Chart.svg/660px-Cosmological_Composition_%E2%80%93_Pie_Chart.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cosmological_Composition_%E2%80%93_Pie_Chart.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pie_chart" title="Pie chart">Chart</a> shows the proportion of different components of the universe &#160;&#8211;  about 95% is <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>During the 1970s and the 1980s, various observations showed that there is not sufficient visible matter in the universe to account for the apparent strength of gravitational forces within and between galaxies. This led to the idea that up to 90% of the matter in the universe is dark matter that does not emit light or interact with normal <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryonic</a> matter. In addition, the assumption that the universe is mostly normal matter led to predictions that were strongly inconsistent with observations. In particular, the universe today is far more lumpy and contains far less deuterium than can be accounted for without dark matter. While dark matter has always been controversial, it is inferred by various observations: the anisotropies in the CMB, <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_cluster" title="Galaxy cluster">galaxy cluster</a> velocity dispersions, large-scale structure distributions, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_lensing" title="Gravitational lensing">gravitational lensing</a> studies, and <a href="/wiki/X-ray_astronomy" title="X-ray astronomy">X-ray measurements</a> of galaxy clusters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Indirect evidence for dark matter comes from its gravitational influence on other matter, as no dark matter particles have been observed in laboratories. Many <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a> candidates for dark matter have been proposed, and several projects to detect them directly are underway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pdg_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-pdg-117">[114]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Additionally, there are outstanding problems associated with the currently favored <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">cold dark matter</a> model which include the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_galaxy_problem" title="Dwarf galaxy problem">dwarf galaxy problem</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[115]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Cuspy_halo_problem" title="Cuspy halo problem">cuspy halo problem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[116]</a></sup> Alternative theories have been proposed that do not require a large amount of undetected matter, but instead modify the laws of gravity established by Newton and Einstein; yet no alternative theory has been as successful as the cold dark matter proposal in explaining all extant observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Horizon_problem">Horizon problem</span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Horizon_problem" title="Horizon problem">horizon problem</a> results from the premise that information cannot travel <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faster_than_light" title="Faster than light">faster than light</a>. In a universe of finite age this sets a limit&#8212;the <a href="/wiki/Particle_horizon" title="Particle horizon">particle horizon</a>&#8212;on the separation of any two regions of space that are in <a href="/wiki/Causality_(physics)" title="Causality (physics)">causal</a> contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kolb_c8_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-kolb_c8-121">[118]</a></sup> The observed isotropy of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">CMB</a> is problematic in this regard: if the universe had been dominated by radiation or matter at all times up to the epoch of last scattering, the particle horizon at that time would correspond to about 2 degrees on the sky. There would then be no mechanism to cause wider regions to have the same temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryden2003_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ryden2003-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>191&#8211;202</span></sup>\n</p><p>A resolution to this apparent inconsistency is offered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inflationary_theory" title="Inflationary theory">inflationary theory</a> in which a homogeneous and isotropic scalar energy field dominates the universe at some very early period (before baryogenesis). During inflation, the universe undergoes exponential expansion, and the particle horizon expands much more rapidly than previously assumed, so that regions presently on opposite sides of the observable universe are well inside each other\'s particle horizon. The observed isotropy of the CMB then follows from the fact that this larger region was in causal contact before the beginning of inflation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guth_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-guth-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>180&#8211;186</span></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heisenberg%27s_uncertainty_principle" title="Heisenberg\'s uncertainty principle">Heisenberg\'s uncertainty principle</a> predicts that during the inflationary phase there would be <a href="/wiki/Primordial_fluctuations" title="Primordial fluctuations">quantum thermal fluctuations</a>, which would be magnified to cosmic scale. These fluctuations serve as the seeds of all current structure in the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ryden2003_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ryden2003-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>207</span></sup> Inflation predicts that the <a href="/wiki/Primordial_fluctuations" title="Primordial fluctuations">primordial fluctuations</a> are nearly <a href="/wiki/Scale_invariance" title="Scale invariance">scale invariant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Normal_distribution" title="Normal distribution">Gaussian</a>, which has been accurately confirmed by measurements of the CMB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>sec 6</span></sup>\n</p><p>If inflation occurred, exponential expansion would push large regions of space well beyond our observable horizon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guth_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-guth-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>180&#8211;186</span></sup>\n</p><p>A related issue to the classic horizon problem arises because in most standard cosmological inflation models, inflation ceases well before <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electroweak_symmetry_breaking" title="Electroweak symmetry breaking">electroweak symmetry breaking</a> occurs, so inflation should not be able to prevent large-scale discontinuities in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electroweak_vacuum" title="Electroweak vacuum">electroweak vacuum</a> since distant parts of the observable universe were causally separate when the <a href="/wiki/Electroweak_epoch" title="Electroweak epoch">electroweak epoch</a> ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[120]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_monopoles">Magnetic monopoles</span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_monopole" title="Magnetic monopole">magnetic monopole</a> objection was raised in the late 1970s. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_unified_theory" title="Grand unified theory">Grand unified theories</a> predicted <a href="/wiki/Topological_defect" title="Topological defect">topological defects</a> in space that would manifest as <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_monopole" title="Magnetic monopole">magnetic monopoles</a>. These objects would be produced efficiently in the hot early universe, resulting in a density much higher than is consistent with observations, given that no monopoles have been found. This problem is also resolved by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_inflation" title="Cosmic inflation">cosmic inflation</a>, which removes all point defects from the observable universe, in the same way that it drives the geometry to flatness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kolb_c8_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-kolb_c8-121">[118]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flatness_problem">Flatness problem</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:End_of_universe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="557" decoding="async" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/End_of_universe.jpg/330px-End_of_universe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/End_of_universe.jpg/495px-End_of_universe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/98/End_of_universe.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:End_of_universe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The overall <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe" title="Shape of the universe">geometry of the universe</a> is determined by whether the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Density_parameter" title="Density parameter">Omega cosmological parameter</a> is less than, equal to or greater than 1. Shown from top to bottom are a <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe#Spherical_universe" title="Shape of the universe">closed universe</a> with positive curvature, a <a href="/wiki/Shape_of_the_universe#Hyperbolic_universe" title="Shape of the universe">hyperbolic universe</a> with negative curvature and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flat_universe" title="Flat universe">flat universe</a> with zero curvature.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Flatness_problem" title="Flatness problem">flatness problem</a> (also known as the oldness problem) is an observational problem associated with a <a href="/wiki/Friedmann%E2%80%93Lema%C3%AEtre%E2%80%93Robertson%E2%80%93Walker_metric" title="Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre&#8211;Robertson&#8211;Walker metric">Friedmann&#8211;Lema&#238;tre&#8211;Robertson&#8211;Walker metric</a> (FLRW).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kolb_c8_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-kolb_c8-121">[118]</a></sup> The universe may have positive, negative, or zero spatial <a href="/wiki/Curvature" title="Curvature">curvature</a> depending on its total energy density. Curvature is negative if its density is less than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_density_(cosmology)" title="Critical density (cosmology)">critical density</a>; positive if greater; and zero at the critical density, in which case space is said to be <i>flat</i>.\n</p><p>The problem is that any small departure from the critical density grows with time, and yet the universe today remains very close to flat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[notes 4]</a></sup> Given that a natural timescale for departure from flatness might be the <a href="/wiki/Planck_time" title="Planck time">Planck time</a>, 10<sup>&#8722;43</sup> seconds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HTUW_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-HTUW-7">[7]</a></sup> the fact that the universe has reached neither a <a href="/wiki/Heat_death_of_the_universe" title="Heat death of the universe">heat death</a> nor a <a href="/wiki/Big_Crunch" title="Big Crunch">Big Crunch</a> after billions of years requires an explanation. For instance, even at the relatively late age of a few minutes (the time of nucleosynthesis), the density of the universe must have been within one part in 10<sup>14</sup> of its critical value, or it would not exist as it does today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[121]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cause">Cause</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_why_there_is_anything_at_all" title="Problem of why there is anything at all">Problem of why there is anything at all</a></div>\n<p>Physics may conclude that <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a> did not exist before \'Big Bang\', but \'started\' with the Big Bang and hence there might be no \'beginning\', \'before\' or potentially \'cause\' and instead always existed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[123]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Quantum_fluctuation" title="Quantum fluctuation">Quantum fluctuations</a>, or other laws of physics that may have existed at the start of the Big Bang could then create the conditions for matter to occur.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ultimate_fate_of_the_universe">Ultimate fate of the universe</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_fate_of_the_universe" title="Ultimate fate of the universe">Ultimate fate of the universe</a></div>\n<p>Before observations of dark energy, cosmologists considered two scenarios for the future of the universe. If the mass <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of the universe were greater than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_density_(cosmology)" title="Critical density (cosmology)">critical density</a>, then the universe would reach a maximum size and then begin to collapse. It would become denser and hotter again, ending with a state similar to that in which it started&#8212;a Big Crunch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kolb_c3_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-kolb_c3-43">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Alternatively, if the density in the universe were equal to or below the critical density, the expansion would slow down but never stop. Star formation would cease with the consumption of interstellar gas in each galaxy; stars would burn out, leaving <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarfs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a>. Very gradually, collisions between these would result in mass accumulating into larger and larger black holes. The average temperature of the universe would asymptotically approach <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a>&#8212;a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_Freeze" title="Big Freeze">Big Freeze</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[124]</a></sup> Moreover, if the proton were <a href="/wiki/Proton_decay" title="Proton decay">unstable</a>, then baryonic matter would disappear, leaving only radiation and black holes. Eventually, black holes would evaporate by emitting <a href="/wiki/Hawking_radiation" title="Hawking radiation">Hawking radiation</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a> of the universe would increase to the point where no organized form of energy could be extracted from it, a scenario known as heat death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dying_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-dying-129">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>sec VI.D</span></sup>\n</p><p>Modern observations of accelerating expansion imply that more and more of the currently visible universe will pass beyond our <a href="/wiki/Event_horizon" title="Event horizon">event horizon</a> and out of contact with us. The eventual result is not known. The &#923;CDM model of the universe contains dark energy in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a>. This theory suggests that only gravitationally bound systems, such as galaxies, will remain together, and they too will be subject to heat death as the universe expands and cools. Other explanations of dark energy, called <a href="/wiki/Phantom_energy" title="Phantom energy">phantom energy</a> theories, suggest that ultimately galaxy clusters, stars, planets, atoms, nuclei, and matter itself will be torn apart by the ever-increasing expansion in a so-called <a href="/wiki/Big_Rip" title="Big Rip">Big Rip</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[126]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Misconceptions">Misconceptions</span></h2>\n<p>The following is a partial list of misconceptions about the Big Bang model:\n</p><p><i>The Big Bang as the origin of the universe:</i> One of the common misconceptions about the Big Bang model is the belief that it was the origin of the universe. However, the Big Bang model does not comment about how the universe came into being. Current conception of the Big Bang model assumes the existence of energy, time, and space, and does not comment about their origin or the cause of the dense and high temperature initial state of the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[127]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The Big Bang was "small"</i>: It is misleading to visualize the Big Bang by comparing its size to everyday objects. When the size of the universe at Big Bang is described, it refers to the size of the observable universe, and not the entire universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-132">[128]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble\'s law">Hubble\'s law</a> violates the <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special theory of relativity</a></i>: Hubble\'s law predicts that galaxies that are beyond <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble\'s law">Hubble Distance</a> recede <a href="/wiki/Faster-than-light" title="Faster-than-light">faster than the speed of light</a>. However, special relativity does not apply beyond motion through space. Hubble\'s law describes velocity that results from expansion <i>of</i> space, rather than <i>through</i> space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-132">[128]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler redshift</a> vs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmological_redshift" title="Cosmological redshift">cosmological red-shift</a></i>: Astronomers&#160;often refer to the cosmological red-shift as a normal Doppler shift,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-132">[128]</a></sup>&#160;which is a misconception. Although similar, the cosmological red-shift is not identical to the Doppler redshift. The Doppler redshift is based on special relativity, which does not consider the expansion of space. On the contrary, the cosmological red-shift is based on general relativity, in which the expansion of space is considered. Although they may appear identical for nearby galaxies, it may cause confusion if the behavior of distant galaxies is understood through the Doppler redshift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_132-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-132">[128]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Speculations">Speculations</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cosmogony" title="Cosmogony">Cosmogony</a></div>\n<p>While the Big Bang model is well established in cosmology, it is likely to be refined. The Big Bang theory, built upon the equations of classical general relativity, indicates a <a href="/wiki/Essential_singularity" title="Essential singularity">singularity</a> at the origin of cosmic time; this <a href="/wiki/Infinity#Physics" title="Infinity">infinite</a> <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> is regarded as impossible in <a href="/wiki/Experimental_physics" title="Experimental physics">physics</a>.  Still, it is known that the equations are not applicable before the time when the universe cooled down to the <a href="/wiki/Planck_temperature" title="Planck temperature">Planck temperature</a>, and this conclusion depends on various assumptions, of which some could never be experimentally verified. <i>(Also see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck_epoch" title="Planck epoch">Planck epoch</a>.)</i>\n</p><p>One proposed refinement to avoid this would-be singularity is to develop a correct treatment of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">quantum gravity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[129]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is not known what could have preceded the hot dense state of the early universe or how and why it originated, though speculation abounds in the field of <a href="/wiki/Cosmogony" title="Cosmogony">cosmogony</a>.\n</p><p>Some proposals, each of which entails untested hypotheses, are:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Models including the <a href="/wiki/Hartle%E2%80%93Hawking_state" title="Hartle&#8211;Hawking state">Hartle&#8211;Hawking no-boundary condition</a>, in which the whole of space-time is finite; the Big Bang does represent the limit of time but without any singularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[130]</a></sup></li>\n<li>Big Bang lattice model, states that the universe at the moment of the Big Bang consists of an infinite lattice of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermions" title="Fermions">fermions</a>, which is smeared over the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_domain" title="Fundamental domain">fundamental domain</a> so it has rotational, translational and gauge symmetry. The symmetry is the largest symmetry possible and hence the lowest entropy of any state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[131]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Brane_cosmology" title="Brane cosmology">Brane cosmology</a> models, in which inflation is due to the movement of branes in <a href="/wiki/String_theory" title="String theory">string theory</a>; the pre-Big Bang model; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ekpyrotic" title="Ekpyrotic">ekpyrotic</a> model, in which the Big Bang is the result of a collision between branes; and the <a href="/wiki/Cyclic_model" title="Cyclic model">cyclic model</a>, a variant of the ekpyrotic model in which collisions occur periodically. In the latter model the Big Bang was preceded by a Big Crunch and the universe cycles from one process to the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rebirth_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-rebirth-138">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rebirth2_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-rebirth2-139">[135]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Eternal_inflation" title="Eternal inflation">Eternal inflation</a>, in which universal inflation ends locally here and there in a random fashion, each end-point leading to a <i>bubble universe</i>, expanding from its own big bang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[137]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Proposals in the last two categories see the Big Bang as an event in either a much larger and <a href="/wiki/Roger_Penrose#An_earlier_universe" title="Roger Penrose">older universe</a> or in a <a href="/wiki/Multiverse" title="Multiverse">multiverse</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_and_philosophical_interpretations">Religious and philosophical interpretations</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_interpretations_of_the_Big_Bang_theory" title="Religious interpretations of the Big Bang theory">Religious interpretations of the Big Bang theory</a></div>\n<p>As a description of the origin of the universe, the Big Bang has significant bearing on religion and philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harris2002_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-harris2002-142">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame2009_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-frame2009-143">[139]</a></sup> As a result, it has become one of the liveliest areas in the discourse between <a href="/wiki/Relationship_between_religion_and_science" title="Relationship between religion and science">science and religion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_-_Theological_Implications_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_-_Theological_Implications-144">[140]</a></sup> Some believe the Big Bang implies a creator,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[142]</a></sup> and some see its mention in their holy books,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asad_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asad-147">[143]</a></sup> while others argue that Big Bang cosmology makes the notion of a creator superfluous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frame2009_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-frame2009-143">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[144]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul universe</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ekpyrotic_universe" title="Ekpyrotic universe"> Ekpyrotic universe</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There is no consensus about how long the Big Bang phase lasted. For some writers, this denotes only the initial singularity, for others the whole history of the universe. Usually, at least the first few minutes (during which helium is synthesized) are said to occur "during the Big Bang".</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Detailed information of and references for tests of general relativity are given in the article <a href="/wiki/Tests_of_general_relativity" title="Tests of general relativity">tests of general relativity</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It is commonly reported that Hoyle intended this to be pejorative. However, Hoyle later denied that, saying that it was just a striking image meant to emphasize the difference between the two theories for radio listeners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-124"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-124">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Strictly, dark energy in the form of a cosmological constant drives the universe towards a flat state; however, our universe remained close to flat for several billion years before the dark energy density became significant.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-NYT-20170220-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-NYT-20170220_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news"><a href="/wiki/Dennis_Overbye" title="Dennis Overbye">Overbye, Dennis</a> (20 February 2017). <a class="external text" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/02/20/science/hubble-constant-universe-expanding-speed.html" rel="nofollow">"Cosmos Controversy: The Universe Is Expanding, but How Fast?"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i><span class="reference-accessdate">.
